    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2475663616764391723"><i>The Floating Opera</i> and <i>The End Of The Road</i> are John Barth's first two novels.  Their relationship to each other is evident not only in their ribald subject matter but in the eccentric characters and bitterly humorous tone of the narratives.  Both concern strange, consuming love triangles and the destructive effect of an overactive intellect on the emotions.  Separately the</span>
  <span id="freeText2475663616764391723" style="display:none"><i>The Floating Opera</i> and <i>The End Of The Road</i> are John Barth's first two novels.  Their relationship to each other is evident not only in their ribald subject matter but in the eccentric characters and bitterly humorous tone of the narratives.  Both concern strange, consuming love triangles and the destructive effect of an overactive intellect on the emotions.  Separately they give two very different views of a universal human drama.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2527602065324672589">I read these two books in college and had the opportunity to meet John Barth at a book signing in the mid-eighties. He looked like a good author should: blazing eyes, etc. I told him I loved the two books so much. He said, "I wrote those when I was just about your age. It would be interesting to see if you liked them so much in twenty years." Then I told him I was going to read The Sot Weed Factor next. He said, "Don't waste your time on more of my books--there are too many good ones out there t</span>
  <span id="freeText2527602065324672589" style="display:none">I read these two books in college and had the opportunity to meet John Barth at a book signing in the mid-eighties. He looked like a good author should: blazing eyes, etc. I told him I loved the two books so much. He said, "I wrote those when I was just about your age. It would be interesting to see if you liked them so much in twenty years." Then I told him I was going to read The Sot Weed Factor next. He said, "Don't waste your time on more of my books--there are too many good ones out there to read." </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" href="/user/show/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis">Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=john-barth-completioniziert">john-barth-completioniziert</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer271737893" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13819041411119858902">According to my Netflix account, the film of "The End of the the Road" will finally (and unfortunately) be released on dvd in September of this year. John Barth has notoriously endorsed the view of critic John Simon whereby it is said that <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>["The principal difference between the novel and the film is that the novel concludes with a harrowing abortion, whereas the film is an abortion from start to finish." <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> "Fairly said, alas," quoth John B. Upon initial release the fi</span>
  <span id="freeText13819041411119858902" style="display:none">According to my Netflix account, the film of "The End of the the Road" will finally (and unfortunately) be released on dvd in September of this year. John Barth has notoriously endorsed the view of critic John Simon whereby it is said that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">["The principal difference between the novel and the film is that the novel concludes with a harrowing abortion, whereas the film is an abortion from start to finish." <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> "Fairly said, alas," quoth John B. Upon initial release the film was X-rated due to scenes not originating from the novel, i.e., "man rapes chicken, etc." Z-rated by the muses. <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0065692/" rel="nofollow noopener">[imdb entry].</a><br /><br /><br /><br />John Barth's first two novels are fine novels. They are good novels. And they should be read before reading his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24842.LETTERS" title="LETTERS by John Barth" rel="nofollow noopener">LETTERS</a>. (Also "The Floating Opera" should be read prior to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24841.Coming_Soon____A_Narrative" title="Coming Soon!!! A Narrative by John Barth" rel="nofollow noopener">Coming Soon!!!: A Narrative</a> but most of you won't get there.) But they are not of that John Barth which we love for all his meta-fictional antics. Start with one of his fat volumes or perhaps Sabbatical if you'd like something a bit more slender. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13819041411119858902" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12093908947742520247">Barth's first two novels in a single edition.<br/><br/><u>The Floating Opera</u><br/><br/>For a first novel, this book has everything -- love in the trenches, a best buddy who shares his hot-wife, a suicide, an attempted suicide, a failed mass murder, a minstrel show, fortunes gained, fortunes lost -- he really packed it in.<br/><br/>Our protagonist is a bit of a nihilist, exposed to all kinds of traumas, his PTSD expresses itself as an aloof attitude and philosophy towards life, as well as physical ailments that torment him for ye</span>
  <span id="freeText12093908947742520247" style="display:none">Barth's first two novels in a single edition.<br /><br /><u>The Floating Opera</u><br /><br />For a first novel, this book has everything -- love in the trenches, a best buddy who shares his hot-wife, a suicide, an attempted suicide, a failed mass murder, a minstrel show, fortunes gained, fortunes lost -- he really packed it in.<br /><br />Our protagonist is a bit of a nihilist, exposed to all kinds of traumas, his PTSD expresses itself as an aloof attitude and philosophy towards life, as well as physical ailments that torment him for years. Eventually, he realizes he can pull the plug on the whole show, and then decides, well, maybe yes, maybe no, maybe maybe.<br /><br />The only thing missing from this book was an ending. I guess you can't have it all.<br /><br /><br /><u>The End of the Road</u><br /><br />Should I stay or should I go now?<br /><br />Jake has issues with decision making, meets a quack, gets a job, meets the proto-EST member in the form of co-worker Joe, accidentally fucks his wife, then works against the clock to prevent a suicide. And again, an unsatisfying ending, but then, it was only his second book.<br /><br /><br />A few years ago I tried and failed to read Barth's Giles Goat-Boy. These two novels are a much better reading experience than GG-B... take my word for it...</span>

<div id="review_3171245372" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3171245372" />
    <a title="Josh Friedlander" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7226831-josh-friedlander"><img alt="Josh Friedlander" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498135749p2/7226831.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3171245372?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh Friedlander" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh Friedlander" href="/user/show/7226831-josh-friedlander">Josh Friedlander</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7226831-josh-friedlander?shelf=existentialist">existentialist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7226831-josh-friedlander?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3171245372" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11768917603856028674">Before the more famous, definitively <i>postmodern</i> novels, Barth began his career with these two short works. I'd classify both as existentialist comic novels, but the second is more of both and more to my taste. They are both about love triangles, and both narrated by smart, amoral characters: the first cavalier and cruel, the second an enigma to himself and us. It is very funny and dry, somewhat reminiscent of Walker Percy. There are philosophical underpinnings but they are not tiresome nor inher</span>
  <span id="freeText11768917603856028674" style="display:none">Before the more famous, definitively <i>postmodern</i> novels, Barth began his career with these two short works. I'd classify both as existentialist comic novels, but the second is more of both and more to my taste. They are both about love triangles, and both narrated by smart, amoral characters: the first cavalier and cruel, the second an enigma to himself and us. It is very funny and dry, somewhat reminiscent of Walker Percy. There are philosophical underpinnings but they are not tiresome nor inherent to the plot; there is a good story in both cases, and Barth's sophisticated, mannered prose has deservedly put him among American fiction's upper echelons.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11768917603856028674" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2114738057" />
    <a title="Simon Robs" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50133154-simon-robs"><img alt="Simon Robs" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479583229p2/50133154.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2114738057?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon Robs" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon Robs" href="/user/show/50133154-simon-robs">Simon Robs</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2114738057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17358582126242868384">Well now Mr. Barth, what on earth were you *thinking* as you composed these twined first go's at novel writing, hmm? I mean it's plain, that is your plotting, setting and tracking worldly events all linear with hardly a misshaped twist or turn. When you go for the psychological throat though, do synch up villainy appropriately, make your anti-hero flawlessly philosophical besides pathological; I know, you did, right. Though you may have moved upon that, if not/maybe enough to pull through an ade</span>
  <span id="freeText17358582126242868384" style="display:none">Well now Mr. Barth, what on earth were you *thinking* as you composed these twined first go's at novel writing, hmm? I mean it's plain, that is your plotting, setting and tracking worldly events all linear with hardly a misshaped twist or turn. When you go for the psychological throat though, do synch up villainy appropriately, make your anti-hero flawlessly philosophical besides pathological; I know, you did, right. Though you may have moved upon that, if not/maybe enough to pull through an adequate denouement in the first book of this tandem "The Floating Opera" - I get the whole suicide setup thing but sociopath's don't generally want to kill *themselves* and your "Toddy boy Andrews" is firmly self serving, but hey what's the quibble, it's all about the making of a writer who when looked back on by reading now, especially having already read his two later, more recognized works, we see the range and movement of artistic locus. These [both books] are "Lost Generation"-type characters with Freudian subplots all, pushed into service drama roles. <br /><br />The second book a dovetail work took the triangle and spun it round a bit this time moving the character sketches closer to home, more real seeming vis. the albeit flip of obviating suicidal imperative, protag., POV. I especially enjoyed the spinoff mad doctor therapy plotline which spiced everything up by off setting the once again overtly infused philosophical dilemma at base of this story. This book kept my attention and set me off associating the way it goes when reader is IN, so three stars for one and maybe four the other - first/second books give rise to others and JB does indeed get the swing of it soon enough!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17358582126242868384" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_107517389" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/107517389" />
    <a title="Bob Arbogast" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3584549-bob-arbogast"><img alt="Bob Arbogast" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/107517389?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Arbogast" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Arbogast" href="/user/show/3584549-bob-arbogast">Bob Arbogast</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of good literature</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer107517389" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11499991408283782085">I have always been a fan of “End of the Road” and now that “The Floating Opera” is experiencing a resurgence, I wanted to read it. As many people have said before me, Barth is a gifted storyteller, and he is intelligent and witty. He is also very good a co-opting literary styles and making them his own, in this case the narrator is a novice writer who through his inexperience breaks narrative conventions as he understand them, and still manages to tell a compelling story.<br/><br/>Todd Andrews is a likabl</span>
  <span id="freeText11499991408283782085" style="display:none">I have always been a fan of “End of the Road” and now that “The Floating Opera” is experiencing a resurgence, I wanted to read it. As many people have said before me, Barth is a gifted storyteller, and he is intelligent and witty. He is also very good a co-opting literary styles and making them his own, in this case the narrator is a novice writer who through his inexperience breaks narrative conventions as he understand them, and still manages to tell a compelling story.<br /><br />Todd Andrews is a likable protagonist, he is charming, attractive, funny, and doesn’t care a flying fig about anything, or so he would lead us to believe. To me, this book is like a stream, when initially the light hits it at a certain angle you can only see the surface and the reflections, but slowly the light shifts and you begin to see beneath the surface, and glimpse new layers and complexities, and finally the light penetrates the water completely, and you can clearly see the life and the movement and the depth of the stream. Said another way, this book was like the movie “Vanya on 42nd St.” in which the director makes you very aware of actors acting, and over the course of the movie, by virtue of the actors talents, you are drawn into the story and the actors are completely lost in their characters. I think it takes a lot of skill to pull this off successfully.<br /><br />It is easy to see why this book was combined with “End of the Road”. They were written in the same year and contain similar themes, but it’s almost like they exist in parallel universes where similar actions result is very different consequences. “End of the Road” was much darker and I liked it better for it, but I read the original version of “The Floating Opera”, with the modified ending, so like would like to read the later release with the “correct” ending, before saying one book is better than the other. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11499991408283782085" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_213753764" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/213753764" />
    <a title="Tom Carson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6125404-tom-carson"><img alt="Tom Carson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329082586p2/6125404.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/213753764?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom Carson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom Carson" href="/user/show/6125404-tom-carson">Tom Carson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer213753764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16729130536535530325">John Barth is the kind of writer who, if you are not a writer, makes you want to write or, if you are trying to be a writer, makes you want to stop completely, as you feel that you could not possibly explore things more thoroughly and eloquently as he can. In short, he is a joy to read.<br/><br/>His characters are at once logical and absurd, universal and absolutely inhuman. They are at once everything that the reader wants to be and everything that he or she finds ridiculous. In this sense, though they m</span>
  <span id="freeText16729130536535530325" style="display:none">John Barth is the kind of writer who, if you are not a writer, makes you want to write or, if you are trying to be a writer, makes you want to stop completely, as you feel that you could not possibly explore things more thoroughly and eloquently as he can. In short, he is a joy to read.<br /><br />His characters are at once logical and absurd, universal and absolutely inhuman. They are at once everything that the reader wants to be and everything that he or she finds ridiculous. In this sense, though they may seem unrealistic at times (and they often do), their deconstruction is made more tragic.<br /><br />His characters may be classically tragic, but his style is thoroughly modern. His mastery of non-sequential time structures makes them always seem logical rather than simply divergent and tangential. His wit and facility of language creates the perfect balance of allowing the reader to simultaneously take the subject matter very seriously and find it absurd.<br /><br />The pairing of these two novels is very appropriate, as reading them together allows the reader to see two very similar themes from two different angles.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16729130536535530325" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_26711619" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26711619" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/863443-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26711619?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/863443-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26711619" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1210102026529747328">Winner of the National Book Award for The Floating Opera, Barth's companion piece, The End of the Road, has the same triangular love affair that the participants try to make workable. The action in both frequently turns out to be quite different than one would expect, the most notable scene being the narrator's initiation into nighttime frontline warfare when an 18 year-old. Completely inexperienced and cut off from contact with anyone with artillery shells whisling overhead, he was terrified un</span>
  <span id="freeText1210102026529747328" style="display:none">Winner of the National Book Award for The Floating Opera, Barth's companion piece, The End of the Road, has the same triangular love affair that the participants try to make workable. The action in both frequently turns out to be quite different than one would expect, the most notable scene being the narrator's initiation into nighttime frontline warfare when an 18 year-old. Completely inexperienced and cut off from contact with anyone with artillery shells whisling overhead, he was terrified until a man jumped into into his water-filled foxhole, and he was so overwhelmed with relief to have another human being within arms reach that he hugged him and wouldn't let go. Even when the man struggled and it became apparent that he was German, he continued to treat him with kindness and the two spent more than hour together before their friendship ends with a twist that the reader doesn't anticipate.. Over and over, Barth's characters do what you'd least expect. That's what makes these two books so interesting. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1210102026529747328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/66869562" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1368291-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1242160041p2/1368291.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/66869562?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/1368291-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer66869562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15956595574625419796">I can't decide if these should be rated/reviewed together or separately. But since I read the books in sucession and they were together in one volume, and clearly linked by subject, I'll rate them together. One is the study of potential suicide in minute forms; the other approaches guilt and irreverence using common neurosis. Both should be about love affairs but both are about much more, so much that the affair shrinks under the weight of everything else...and somehow, they aren't about love at</span>
  <span id="freeText15956595574625419796" style="display:none">I can't decide if these should be rated/reviewed together or separately. But since I read the books in sucession and they were together in one volume, and clearly linked by subject, I'll rate them together. One is the study of potential suicide in minute forms; the other approaches guilt and irreverence using common neurosis. Both should be about love affairs but both are about much more, so much that the affair shrinks under the weight of everything else...and somehow, they aren't about love at all. I loved both but the second one more than the first, and aside from the writing technicalities or the stories or whatever, I really liked the way the time periods came out in each - subtle, subtle, subtle. Thanks, DB, for an awesome b-day present. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15956595574625419796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2547385579" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2547385579" />
    <a title="Reid" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17530370-reid"><img alt="Reid" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1538493792p2/17530370.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2547385579?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Reid" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Reid" href="/user/show/17530370-reid">Reid</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17530370-reid?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2547385579" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13221675286889930213">This review focuses on The Floating Opera rather than The End of the Road, which is an altogether bleak and unenjoyable text, while The Floating Opera is bleak but readable. <br/><br/>Todd Andrews likes to think of himself as a reasonable man. The son of a successful lawyer and the sort of WASP that dominated the 20th-century Chesapeake Bay Area, Andrews has no reason to be discontent. But two events cause him to begin to deconstruct his morality: the discovery of a life-threatening illness and his father</span>
  <span id="freeText13221675286889930213" style="display:none">This review focuses on The Floating Opera rather than The End of the Road, which is an altogether bleak and unenjoyable text, while The Floating Opera is bleak but readable. <br /><br />Todd Andrews likes to think of himself as a reasonable man. The son of a successful lawyer and the sort of WASP that dominated the 20th-century Chesapeake Bay Area, Andrews has no reason to be discontent. But two events cause him to begin to deconstruct his morality: the discovery of a life-threatening illness and his father's suicide. Through the course of The Floating Opera, Andrews increasingly deconstructs his values, to the point where he discovers that there is no intrinsic meaning whatsoever, and it does not matter if he lives or dies. <br /><br />The Floating Opera is one of those post-modern philosophical novels where the narrator carries on about how everything is meaningless and acts in a narcissistic and hostile way towards others because he (and it's always a he) can. The book is truly a bleak approach to morality, and one that questions whether there is a need for philosophical questioning at all. If we can deconstruct morality all the way to nothingness, can we not deconstruct deconstruction as well?</span>
  <a data-text-id="13221675286889930213" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2940939232" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2940939232" />
    <a title="Rossrn Nunamaker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1044243-rossrn-nunamaker"><img alt="Rossrn Nunamaker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1250539590p2/1044243.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2940939232?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rossrn Nunamaker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rossrn Nunamaker" href="/user/show/1044243-rossrn-nunamaker">Rossrn Nunamaker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1044243-rossrn-nunamaker?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2940939232" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2836824880988020675">I was first introduced to John Barth in HS when we were assigned to read The Floating Opera.<br/><br/>Our teacher gave us the book and the first reading assignment and told us all not to act on anything we read, but instead to be ready to discuss it tomorrow in class.<br/><br/>My father, seeing the book, and knowing it was taught at the school, as he was also a teacher, mentioned that he had John Barth as his Freshman Comp teacher at Penn State. He never understood as a then Engineering student how he wound up in t</span>
  <span id="freeText2836824880988020675" style="display:none">I was first introduced to John Barth in HS when we were assigned to read The Floating Opera.<br /><br />Our teacher gave us the book and the first reading assignment and told us all not to act on anything we read, but instead to be ready to discuss it tomorrow in class.<br /><br />My father, seeing the book, and knowing it was taught at the school, as he was also a teacher, mentioned that he had John Barth as his Freshman Comp teacher at Penn State. He never understood as a then Engineering student how he wound up in that particular class (together we met him during his book tour for The Last Voyage of Somebody the Sailor).<br /><br />The book along with the family connection and of course the writing made me a fan.<br /><br />I greatly enjoyed the setting of Maryland's Eastern Shore and I found both stories to be extremely thought provoking, particularly at that stage of my life.<br /><br />Like any writer, Barth is not for everyone. While I have read many of his works, I have not read all of them.<br /><br />I do feel that these two, particularly the Floating Opera, is a good litmus test as to whether or not you should try reading more of his work.<br /><br />The ending of The Floating Opera may have left many disappointing, but I found it satisfying.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2836824880988020675" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3595988028" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3595988028" />
    <a title="Lee Holden" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34025731-lee-holden"><img alt="Lee Holden" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3595988028?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Holden" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Holden" href="/user/show/34025731-lee-holden">Lee Holden</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3595988028" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3861112006040245517">Floating Opera - Funny, but very dry. I started the book maybe three or four weeks ago, and finished it yesterday. Which isn't a great pace, considering it's 252 pages long. I ended up reading in big chunks, then not touching it for days. For me, it mainly had to do with the lack of compelling plot. The most compelling parts were the sections in which Todd narrates his past, particularly the World War I scene. The writing was fun. It felt like Barth (who I imagine as this witty, hyper-intelligen</span>
  <span id="freeText3861112006040245517" style="display:none">Floating Opera - Funny, but very dry. I started the book maybe three or four weeks ago, and finished it yesterday. Which isn't a great pace, considering it's 252 pages long. I ended up reading in big chunks, then not touching it for days. For me, it mainly had to do with the lack of compelling plot. The most compelling parts were the sections in which Todd narrates his past, particularly the World War I scene. The writing was fun. It felt like Barth (who I imagine as this witty, hyper-intelligent prune of a man) was spinning this yarn in front of me. Very personable. Despite being short, I just couldn't build momentum, so I have to knock it down some notches for that. In the end, it's another novel about nothing happening, with enough humor to interest you in finishing. All I can hope for is that his work improved as he aged, otherwise his longer novels will be an absolute slog.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3861112006040245517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3156897467" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3156897467" />
    <a title="Jeff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/82329-jeff"><img alt="Jeff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572980323p2/82329.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3156897467?book_show_action=true">Feb 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff" href="/user/show/82329-jeff">Jeff</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3156897467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18081452575573799284">The Floating Opera and The End of The Road are John Barth's first two novels repackaged in one volume. Both novels are about a love triangle and the contemplation of suicide. The Floating Opera is set in the early 1930s and is a novel-length explanation of why the narrator decided to kill himself one day in 1937 and the events that changed his mind. The End of the Road is contemporary to the time it was written (1954) and also considers the question of suicide. <br/><br/>While The Floating Opera kept my i</span>
  <span id="freeText18081452575573799284" style="display:none">The Floating Opera and The End of The Road are John Barth's first two novels repackaged in one volume. Both novels are about a love triangle and the contemplation of suicide. The Floating Opera is set in the early 1930s and is a novel-length explanation of why the narrator decided to kill himself one day in 1937 and the events that changed his mind. The End of the Road is contemporary to the time it was written (1954) and also considers the question of suicide. <br /><br />While The Floating Opera kept my interest, I though The End of the Road was a complete waste of time. Floating Opera gets the three stars and End of the Road—zero. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_144642090" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/144642090" />
    <a title="C. Quabela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2606780-c-quabela"><img alt="C. Quabela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540861330p2/2606780.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/144642090?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="C. Quabela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="C. Quabela" href="/user/show/2606780-c-quabela">C. Quabela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2606780-c-quabela?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2606780-c-quabela?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2606780-c-quabela?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer144642090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3186828004412157386">My first experience with John Barth was with his collection of short stories "Lost in the Fun House." I was magnified by his finesse with language and, although I didn't feel that all of those stories were successes (and what writer's ever are?), he left me spellbound as to the potential of future fiction. Having come across him through one of my favorite authors David Foster Wallace and his story "Westward the Course of Empire Takes it's Way," which was a response to Barth's short story from wh</span>
  <span id="freeText3186828004412157386" style="display:none">My first experience with John Barth was with his collection of short stories "Lost in the Fun House." I was magnified by his finesse with language and, although I didn't feel that all of those stories were successes (and what writer's ever are?), he left me spellbound as to the potential of future fiction. Having come across him through one of my favorite authors David Foster Wallace and his story "Westward the Course of Empire Takes it's Way," which was a response to Barth's short story from which the prior collection derived it's name, I needed to get more!<br /><br />This was not a disappointment! It is never easy to tell if a writer of short stories (which was how I became introduced to him) would be a good novelist. Nevertheless I found these two stories just as spellbinding. As his first two attempts at becoming an author, they of course have their juvenile aspects to them and conventions that seemed forced - but I personally feel every author should be forgiven that in their early career when attempting to find their voice. <br /><br />His ability to develop a protagonist of solid depth though is incredible. During a time when other authors were writing about the active individual in a rapidly individualizing time in American letters, Barth was able to create two characters of non-action; a progenitor to future meta-fiction. Although the anti-climax had already begun to emerge in literature, these two stories have the courage to force it upon the reader whereas others had only really had a dwindling down without exploiting the fact that the modern text need not satisfy, in fact must not satisfy, in a unsatisfactory world. I would even place these two novellas along-side Robert Musil's epic "The Man Without Qualities." Any extensive reader can admit that every topic has been written about - which is true - but what makes literature is it's ability to keep those "truths" of humanity alive in the collective consciousness in it's ever changing forms, which I feel these two stories effectively do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3186828004412157386" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_949627068" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/949627068" />
    <a title="Dustin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3903811-dustin"><img alt="Dustin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325730379p2/3903811.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/949627068?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dustin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dustin" href="/user/show/3903811-dustin">Dustin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer949627068" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14936966170779850958">I didn't know much about these going in, so I was unprepared for how much there was to swallow, philosophically speaking. I want to read it again right away (though I won't), because I wasn't really ready for it. I wasn't reading quite the right way. After I realized what I'd gotten myself into and got my feet under me, it was better, but for a moment there it was a scramble of recontextualization. <br/><br/>Anyway. It has a heavy nihilist streak, and flat out makes a lot of claims about Life. I had some </span>
  <span id="freeText14936966170779850958" style="display:none">I didn't know much about these going in, so I was unprepared for how much there was to swallow, philosophically speaking. I want to read it again right away (though I won't), because I wasn't really ready for it. I wasn't reading quite the right way. After I realized what I'd gotten myself into and got my feet under me, it was better, but for a moment there it was a scramble of recontextualization. <br /><br />Anyway. It has a heavy nihilist streak, and flat out makes a lot of claims about Life. I had some trouble ingesting some of the ideas, because most of the philosophising comes from the perspective of the protagonists, and the protagonists are at once both direly austere and at the same time absurd. It would go from spinning out some well reasoned thought and then follow it up by making them totally unrelatable. I generally like to have a grasp on what I think the author is trying to impart to me the reader. I can still do what I want with that message, but I like to understand what it is at least. At first I thought I was dealing with the treacherous unreliable narrator, and while that may still be the case generally, I don't think it's the case specifically. I also don't think that the character is being used as a means to comment on the ideas, instead of presenting them.<br /><br />I don't know, it was super interesting and I really liked it. I guess it just boils down to being interested in what was being said while maybe not understanding who was saying it. Although, I will say, there were parts of the characters that I absolutely understood, and maybe I'm having some discomfort from those things being packaged along with some things that I don't want to relate to, or being made to realize that I might.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14936966170779850958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_145009484" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145009484" />
    <a title="Alb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/358322-alb"><img alt="Alb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1260329129p2/358322.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/145009484?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alb" href="/user/show/358322-alb">Alb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/358322-alb?shelf=best-of-2011">best-of-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer145009484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8552664350848918053">I first heard about this book in one of my undergrad philosophy classes. I always thought of it as a philosophical treatise and felt a little intimidated by it. As I was looking back on the books I read in 2010, I realized I had read a lot of pulpy fiction and was feeling like I should add something with a little more mental nutrition to my night stand. With this goal in mind, I decided to go to the Floating Opera. While I feel like I did get the intellectual stimulation I felt I needed, I was i</span>
  <span id="freeText8552664350848918053" style="display:none">I first heard about this book in one of my undergrad philosophy classes. I always thought of it as a philosophical treatise and felt a little intimidated by it. As I was looking back on the books I read in 2010, I realized I had read a lot of pulpy fiction and was feeling like I should add something with a little more mental nutrition to my night stand. With this goal in mind, I decided to go to the Floating Opera. While I feel like I did get the intellectual stimulation I felt I needed, I was immediately surprised by how readable the book was. Although, it is tackling big themes such as the nature of grief and the intrinsic value of life it is also an engaging narrative told by a charming and somewhat sympathetic narrator. Although, I do wish I would have had the opportunity to have read this book in an English or Philosophy class because this is one of those books that I think would be enhanced by a guided discussion. I was particularly taken by the unconventional structure and unique narrative device. <br /><br />The literary experiment WIttengenstein's Mistress that I read a few years ago came to mind when I first started the Floating Opera. In both books, the narrator is unreliable and speaking to the reader directly. What a feel makes the Floating Opera work where Wittengenstein's Mistress failed for me was that the Floating Opera had narrative thread. Although Todd rambled a bit and wasn't the most likable of people, I still felt invested in him as a character. When I have a little more time I hope to pick this edition and again and read the follow up book The End of the Road.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8552664350848918053" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1286060774" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1286060774" />
    <a title="Jeff Buddle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23285240-jeff-buddle"><img alt="Jeff Buddle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377092443p2/23285240.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1286060774?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff Buddle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff Buddle" href="/user/show/23285240-jeff-buddle">Jeff Buddle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1286060774" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4976861444254452314">Hmm. John Barth. Are people still reading him? They should be. These two novels are twined together thematically as well as physically. Each has a bizarre love triangle. In each, a character mediates on the idea of suicide. Both are short, but feel rich. Barth cobbles together his novels in discrete chunks, little stories within the story that eventually reveal the whole; sometimes they're chunks of stories distributed evenly throughout the novel. <br/><br/>I believe this is how Barth builds a page-turner</span>
  <span id="freeText4976861444254452314" style="display:none">Hmm. John Barth. Are people still reading him? They should be. These two novels are twined together thematically as well as physically. Each has a bizarre love triangle. In each, a character mediates on the idea of suicide. Both are short, but feel rich. Barth cobbles together his novels in discrete chunks, little stories within the story that eventually reveal the whole; sometimes they're chunks of stories distributed evenly throughout the novel. <br /><br />I believe this is how Barth builds a page-turner out of what might otherwise be a slog. In "The Floating Opera" we're introduced to the titular opera and reminded of it throughout the novel. The narrator wants to attend, but it's not something he mediates on, just remembers here and there. When he finally arrives at the opera, the role it plays is a bigger surprise than you expected.<br /><br />It's clear from reading these novels that Barth's influence is still pretty powerful. I don't think that contemporary writers like Ben Lerner, Tom McCarthy and Valeria Luiselli would exist without him. The metafiction in these two novels is pretty low-level stuff compared to what is to come for Barth, but the self-aware narrators, the concept that what you're reading is a product of an author, and the awareness of the author that you're engaging with the book as a reader are enough to define the parallels between Barth's work and this younger set.<br /><br />In the end, "Sot-Weed" is a far more compelling book. It dazzles where these novels sparkle. However, these books should not be ignored or overlooked. These are the books I'd put next to Lerner, McCarthy and Luiselli to understand where they're coming from. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4976861444254452314" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

These essays offer brilliant commentary and outrageous humor, in signature Thompson style.Ranging in date from the National Observer days to the era of Rolling Stone, The Great Shark Hunt offers myriad, highly charged entries, including the first Hunter S. Thompson piece to be dubbed “gonzo”—“The Kentucky Derby Is Decadent and Depraved,” which appeared in Scanlan\'s Monthly in 1970. From this essay a new journalistic movement sprang which would change the shape of American letters. Baba Booey!&quot; It was a slip of the tongue--that unfortunately was heard by a few million listeners--but in that split second a nickname, a persona, a rallying cry, and a phenomenon was born. Some would say it was the moment Gary Dell\'Abate, the long-suffering heroic producer of The Howard Stern Show, for better or worse, finally came into his own. In &quot;They Call Me Baba Booey,&quot; Dell\'Abate explains how his early life was the perfect training ground for the day-to-day chaos that comes with producing the most popular radio show on earth. Growing up on Long Island in the 1970s, the youngest of three boys born to a clinically depressed mother, Gary learned how to fend for himself when under attack. Obsessed with music, he listened with religious intensity to Casey Kasem\'s Top 40 every Sunday morning, compulsively bought 45s of his favorite songs, and nerdily copied the lyrics into a notebook. Music became an ordering principle to his life, even as the chaos at home got out of hand. Dell\'Abate\'s memoir sketches the trajectory from the obsessive pop-music trivia buff to the man in the beekeeper\'s mask who handily defeats his opponents playing &quot;Stump the Booey.&quot; We learn about the memorable moments in his life that taught him to endure epic bouts of humiliation and get his unique perspective on some of his favorite Stern show episodes--such as the day he nearly killed the Mets mascot while throwing out the first pitch, or the time his mother called Howard\'s mother and demanded an apology. With the help and support of friends and family, Lange manages to recover and get his life and career back on track. We created technologies that would help connect us faster, gather news, map the planet, compile knowledge, and connect with anyone, at anytime. We strove for an instantaneous network where time and space could be compressed.\nWell, the future\'s arrived. We live in a continuous now enabled by Twitter, email, and a so-called real-time technological shift. Yet this now is an elusive goal that we can never quite reach. And the dissonance between our digital selves and our analog bodies has thrown us into a new state of anxiety: present shock.\nRushkoff weaves together seemingly disparate events and trends into a rich, nuanced portrait of how life in the eternal present has affected our biology, behavior, politics, and culture. He explains how the rise of zombie apocalypse fiction signals our intense desire for an ending; how the Tea Party and Occupy Wall Street form two sides of the same post-narrative coin; how corporate investing in the future has been replaced by futile efforts to game the stock market in real time; why social networks make people anxious and email can feel like an assault. He examines how the tragedy of 9/11 disconnected an entire generation from a sense of history, and delves into why conspiracy theories actually comfort us.\nAs both individuals and communities, we have a choice. We can struggle through the onslaught of information and play an eternal game of catch-up. Or we can choose to live in the present: favor eye contact over texting; quality over speed; and human quirks over digital perfection. Rushkoff offers hope for anyone seeking to transcend the false now.\nAbsorbing and thought-provoking, Present Shock is a wide-ranging, deeply thought meditation on what it means to be human in       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8113.John_Barth">About John Barth</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8113.John_Barth"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1222685060p3/8113.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/8113.John_Barth">John Barth
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
536
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7278933962072465522">John Simmons Barth is an American novelist and short-story writer, known for the postmodernist and metafictive quality of his work.<br/><br/>John Barth was born in Cambridge, Maryland, and briefly studied "Elementary Theory and Advanced Orchestration" at Juilliard before attending Johns Hopkins University, receiving a B.A. in 1951 and an M.A. in 1952 (for which he wrote a thesis novel, The Shirt of Nessus).</span>
  <span id="freeText7278933962072465522" style="display:none">John Simmons Barth is an American novelist and short-story writer, known for the postmodernist and metafictive quality of his work.<br /><br />John Barth was born in Cambridge, Maryland, and briefly studied "Elementary Theory and Advanced Orchestration" at Juilliard before attending Johns Hopkins University, receiving a B.A. in 1951 and an M.A. in 1952 (for which he wrote a thesis novel, The Shirt of Nessus).<br /><br />He was a professor at Penn State University (1953-1965), SUNY Buffalo (1965-1973), Boston University (visiting professor, 1972-1973), and Johns Hopkins University (1973-1995) before he retired in 1995.<br /><br />Barth began his career with The Floating Opera and The End of the Road, two short novels that deal wittily with controversial topics, suicide and abortion respectively. They are straightforward tales; as Barth later remarked, they "didn't know they were novels."<br /><br />The Sot-Weed Factor, Barth's next novel, is an 800-page mock epic of the colonization of Maryland based on the life of an actual poet, Ebenezer Cooke, who wrote a poem of the same title. The Sot-Weed Factor is what Northrop Frye called an anatomy — a large, loosely structured work, with digressions, distractions, stories within stories, and lists (such as a lengthy exchange of insulting terms by two prostitutes). The fictional Ebenezer Cooke (repeatedly described as "poet and virgin") is a Candide-like innocent who sets out to write a heroic epic, becomes disillusioned and ends up writing a biting satire.<br /><br />Barth's next novel, Giles Goat-Boy, of comparable size, is a speculative fiction based on the conceit of the university as universe. A half-man, half-goat discovers his humanity and becomes a savior in a story presented as a computer tape given to Barth, who denies that it is his work. In the course of the novel Giles carries out all the tasks prescribed by Joseph Campbell in The Hero with a Thousand Faces. Barth kept a list of the tasks taped to his wall while he was writing the book.<br /><br />The short story collection Lost in the Funhouse and the novella collection Chimera are even more metafictional than their two predecessors, foregrounding the writing process and presenting achievements such as seven nested quotations. In LETTERS Barth and the characters of his first six books interact.<br /><br />While writing these books, Barth was also pondering and discussing the theoretical problems of fiction writing, most notably in an essay, "The Literature of Exhaustion" (first printed in the Atlantic, 1967), that was widely considered to be a statement of "the death of the novel" (compare with Roland Barthes's "The Death of the Author"). Barth has since insisted that he was merely making clear that a particular stage in history was passing, and pointing to possible directions from there. He later (1979) wrote a follow-up essay, "The Literature of Replenishment," to clarify the point.<br /><br />Barth's fiction continues to maintain a precarious balance between postmodern self-consciousness and wordplay on the one hand, and the sympathetic characterisation and "page-turning" plotting commonly associated with more traditional genres and subgenres of classic and contemporary storytelling.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7278933962072465522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
